0743277457|4.0|INTRODUCTION<br /><br />Waitzkin was a chess prodigy, dominating the scholastic chess world for ten years. He picked up Tai Chi and became so proficient within a few years that he became World Champion in 'Push Hands', the martial aspect of Tai Chi. He realized that competitive chess psychology could readily be applied in Tai Chi, and that his insights from Tai Chi also influenced his chessplay. Waitzkin contends that there are many principles of learning and performance common to many disciplines, even outwardly unrelated ones like Chess and Tai Chi. This book weaves these principles into his riveting autobiography of his competitive careers. His illustrations draw from his competitive experience, but his ideas are probably useful in general.<br /><br />ESSENCE<br /><br />Make your work an expression of your innate nature to 'stay true' to your art and avoid alienating your art from your character. [pages 9, 41, 80]<br /><br />Children who are praised using \\"you're so smart\\" can become 'Entity Learners' who easily give up on anything they're not really good at. They believe that to be good in something, you need to have this quality ('entity') of talent in it. 'Incremental Learners', on the other hand, thrive on praise like \\"keep up the good work\\" because they believe they can master anything by working hard. In the face of failure, Incremental Learners raise their game while Entity Learners run away and lose a growth opportunity. [30, 44]<br /><br />Learning is an alternating sequence of static, safe mediocrity and vulnerable growth periods. When fixing our flaws or trying new techniques, our ability weakens temporarily ('Investment in Loss'), which Incremental Learners can endure but Entity Learners avoid. [33, 107]<br /><br />Many understand that having zero distractions helps one concentrate better. That is termed the 'Hard Zone', which unfortunately is not always a possible environment to maintain when we need to concentrate. A much better alternative is the 'Soft Zone', were we incorporate distractions, including our emotions, into our thoughts, using them as fuel for creative insights. For instance, tunes in Waitzkin's head disrupted his chessplay until he thought to the beat of the music, allowing the music to influence his style of play creatively. [54, 126, 207]<br /><br />Techniques and knowledge can be internalized via much practice, something like 'carving neural pathways'. [138] Eventually, internalized skills are integrated into the 'unconscious skillset' that we wield powerfully without knowing. [140, 231]<br /><br />If one grasps the essence behind a skill, he can repeatedly practice the reduction of the actions that implement the skill, yet retain the essence ('Making Smaller Circles'). Eventually, powerful skills can become nearly invisible, such as Bruce Lee's sledgehammer 'six-inch punch'. [121]<br /><br />By focusing on a small data set (e.g. a certain hand of a fighting opponent), our brain can process things in higher frames-per-second, 'slowing down time'. This can be done without ignoring the rest of the data; the rest is left to the 'unconscious skillset' to deal with instinctively. [146] Waitzkin has used this to attack opponents when they are in the midst of blinking.<br /><br />The highest stage of any competitive sport is psychological warfare - the reading and controlling of intention. To quote a 19th-century sage on Tai Chi, \\"If the opponent does not move, then I do not move. At the opponent's slightest move, I move first.\\" - Wu Yu-hsiang [149]<br /><br />We occasionally enter moments of incredible presence when we are very focused and aware. 'Masters of living' maximize their creative potential by cultivating constant presence at every moment in life. This cultivates extra presence in sudden stressful situations. [172] Constant awareness also allows us to see beauty in mundane things that we usually ignore. [197]<br /><br />The greatest competitors know how to recover effectively between periods of stress. For instance, Michael Jordan completely ignores his basketball match during a two-minute break on the bench. [179]<br /><br />Entering a 'Zone' gives you higher performance, so we can 'Build Our Trigger' to the Zone. Waitzkin helped this guy Dennis create one: Dennis recognized that playing catch with his son made him feel relaxed and present in the moment, so Waitzkin built a routine around that, using activities that Dennis was already used to. Before playing catch, Dennis would eat a certain light snack for 10 mins, meditate for 15 mins, do stretching for 10 mins and listen to 10 mins of Bob Dylan. After a month of this routine, he did this routine before important meetings and became much more serene and productive during work. Waitzkin the slowly guided him in shortening his routine ('Making Smaller Circles'), eventually removing the snack and condensing the rest to 12 mins, yet retaining its effect. The routine, constructed to Dennis's tastes, became physiologically connected to a serene state of mind which can now be triggered. [188]<br /><br />Bring all the above skills together! [howto: 225]<br /><br />Creativity is relative to a certain foundation of knowledge. Moments of divine inspiration give us glimpses of something miraculous, and most are contented to hope for more of such inspiring moments. Waitzkin contends that we must investigate and find out `what makes the magic tick'. This raises our foundation to a whole new level, where what seemed like divine insight previously are common sense to us now. The cycle then continues with even greater inspirations and the struggle to make sense of them. [231]<br /><br />AFTERTHOUGHTS<br /><br />I didn't know Tai Chi could become so violent! Waitzkin once faced an opponent who was so good at reading his intention that seemed to read his mind. Waitzkin countered by rehearsing fake moves in his head so his opponent would gaurd against something nonexistant, while opening a loophole for Waitzkin to take advantage of.<br /><br />I must quote Waitzkin's romantic description of his first encounter with chess:<br /><br />\\"I was six years old, a rough-and-tumble kid... driving my parents crazy with mischief. 'Too much boy,' my mom says... I loved to swing on the monkey bars and become Tarzan, the world my jungle. But now something seemed different. I looked over my shoulder, and was transfixed by mysterious figurines set up on a marble chessboard. I remember feeling I was looking into a forest. The pieces were animals, filled with strange potential, as if something dangerous and magical were about the leap from the board... then the pieces exploded into action, nimble fingers [of old men playing in the park]... I was pulled into the battlefield, enraptured; something felt familiar about this game, it made sense... it was a strange feeling of discovering a lost memory.\\" [3]<br /><br />This raises two points:<br /><br />I. Talent really exists.<br />II. In contrast to the usual encouragement to \\"quiet our mind\\" and \\"be in the present moment\\" when appreciating fine art, the beauty of Chess (and other games of strategy) is not in any \\"present state\\", but the *potential* of the complex magic that can erupt from the quiet and deceptively docile battlefield.

0691152640|4.0|The historical perspective on government debt defaults is invaluable to those in finance and investing.  This should be required material in business education (at least the data).  Modern Finance is built on the notion that government debt is risk-free (at least the US's), which is rubbish.  Love the historical perspective and insightful charts that neatly summarize their findings, but I found the book repetitive, verbose and a bit \\"academic\\" (in the sense of naive).  The authors point out the meaningful data limitations, such as those on domestic debt default, but then seem to ignore them in their statistical analysis (such as whether there's more tendency to default on ext vs dom debt).  I also quibble with some of the details of their analysis such as grouping serial defaults (those within 2 years of each other) into one.  It is important for a stakeholder to have some sense of the likelihood of further defaults in the short-term given one has occurred. That said, I found this book to have been well worth the read.<br /><br />One glaring omission is \\"off-balance\\" sheet financing such as leasing, entitlements, and long-term purchasing commitments.  Inclusion of debt-like obligations would change the financial picture dramatically and enable better comparisons across time and countries.  After all, some countries are more reliant on off-balance sheet financing than others.

It's brilliant.<br /><br />It was written in a way that plays on our unconscious fears of technology and social norms as we humans spiral towards our future, using the the lubrication of technology to radically propel us violently towards social change. What then? We can't be less resilient than the cockroach, the majority-agreed living being on this planet voted most likely to succeed. Howey must agree because his fast-forward humanity takes place in a time still reactionary to events that changed the face of the planet, even though those in that time don't realize it.<br /><br />It's wonderful because it starts out with describing 'the way things are'. Then it goes to conflicts that happen because of the way things are. Then there are slow reveals to WHY things are they way things are. Then it moves to climactic finale in the idea of social revolution... thousands of years beyond our current day. I like this because the way he writes, he isn't inserting items and scenarios that are fantastical. More, he writes about things that are futuristic mutations of what we already know. This makes it chilling and personal.<br /><br />All throughout I ask myself, am I a person who made this world? Am I a person who could survive in it?<br /><br />I like that.ngs that are futuristic mutations of what we already know. He also had a beautiful daughter who was nowhere to be seen, and not even mentioned, in the movie.  6 - The movie intimated that the Therns were aliens to Mars. They were definitely not Holy, and Issus turned out to be a several hundred year old crazy woman who dined on the &#34;delicate&#34; parts of the most beautiful women on the planet that happened to be taken hostage by the Black Pirates of Barsoom (who themselves made raids on the Therns).  7 - Dajah Thoris was not a warrior, and neither was she a great scholar. She was a smart, pretty, and determined little princess who could wield a sword if she had too, and that was about it.  8 - John Carter could jump at most around 150 feet. Most of the time it was more like 10-50 feet. The movie has him jumping a thousand feet or more.  9 - Tardas Mors (her grand-father in the books) would never have made Dajah marry &#34;Sab Than&#34;, no matter what the cost to the city of Helium. There is no mention of Mors Kajak (her father in the books) at all.  10 - Powell and Carter were mining partners in the books.  11 - Sab Than was not a Jeddak and the city of Zodanga didn't move around on a giant machine. This was just stupid.  12 - If Kantos Kan had talked down to Dejah the way he did at the first of this movie, (Basically telling her to shut up) he would have gotten the hell beat out of him.  13 - The final insult was when Stanton changed the greeting &#34;Kaor&#34; to &#34;Gayor&#34; He totally gayed it up. I understand why it didn't do well in theaters. The early reviews were horribleand the previews really didn't let people know what the move was about. B005LAIHKY|5.0|Tim Burton's Dark Shadows 2012 (DS12) ranks right up there for me with Mel Brooks's 1974 masterpiece, [[ASIN:B000G6BLWE Young Frankenstein]], which is a hilarious parody of Universal Studios's horror movies of the 1930's, something that DS12 does, at least indirectly, as well. I personally rank this show 4 stars for script, 5 stars for CGI special effects, 5 stars for the elaborate sets by production designer Rick Heinrichs, and 5 stars for the beautiful costumes designed by Colleen Atwood. Most of all I give 5+ stars to the actors, in particular Johnny Depp. His acting is always wonderful, but it is a real joy for anyone who loves comedy to see him in a comedic part. He is a master at subtle nuances of expression, making him outstanding as either the one delivering the comic lines, or the straight man receiving them. To me, he is the best part of this film, but it is also a great pleasure to see so many talented supporting actors in the film, especially Eva Green, whose performance is terrific as the villain, Angelique Bouchard, an evil witch.<br /><br />The afternoon soap opera, [[ASIN:B007PZ6SYK Dark Shadows]] (DSTV), on which this film is based, ran on network TV from 1966-1971. Its central character is a handsome vampire, Barnabas Collins, who was portrayed with equal parts anguish and menace by talented actor, Jonathan Frid. His central role is reprised in DS12 by Depp, who has a long, successful history with the film's director, Tim Burton (a partial list of their teamwork includes, [[ASIN:B00004U8P8 Edward Scissorhands]], [[ASIN:B0000VD04M Ed Wood]], [[ASIN:B001BN1ZHW Sweeney Todd]], Corpse Bride, Charlie and the Chocolate Factory, [[ASIN:0792164903 Sleepy Hollow]], and [[ASIN:B001HN694K Alice in Wonderland]]). During the era it was broadcast, DSTV offered its audience an excitingly bizarre mishmash of vampires, witches and werewolves like nothing anyone had ever seen on TV or even in films. In their youth, both Burton and Depp were big fans of the show, and Burton claims it had a major impact on the type of films he would make later in life.<br /><br />DS12 does not try to repeat what DSTV accomplished but rather expands on it. In a fun homage, DS12 is set in the same era as the TV series itself, beginning in 1972, the year after DSTV ended. Burton draws on the classic American tale, Rip Van Winkle, for his plot (a story written by Washington Irving, the 19th-century author on whose work Burton based his film, Sleepy Hollow). Like poor Rip, Barnabas (Depp) is condemned to a long \\"sleep.\\" In his case, the evil witch Angelique, in punishment for rich, 18th-century playboy Barnabas's breaking her heart, turns him into a vampire and buries him in a coffin for a sleep ten times as long as Rip's enchanted slumber in a mountain meadow. Like Rip, when Barnabas escapes his magical imprisonment, he discovers the world he knew has vanished. Instead of staid 1772 (which is also Rip's era), it is now psychedelic, disco-ridden 1972. Barnabas seeks out his ancestral home and learns that the once magnificent estate is now in ruins, and his seriously dysfunctional descendants are strongly in need of his assistance to rebuild the family fortune. They include matriarch Elizabeth Collins Stoddard (Michelle Pfeifer), her debauched brother Roger (Johnny Lee Miller), her insolent teenage daughter Carolyn (Chloe Grace Moretz), and Roger's unstable, young son David (Gulliver/Gully McGrath). The household also includes a meek governess, Victoria Winters (Bella Heathcote), an alcoholic caretaker, Willie (Jackie Earle Haley), a senile servant, Mrs. Johnson (Ray Shirley), and a drunken psychiatrist, Dr. Julia Hoffman (Helena Bonham Carter who, as Tim Burton's wife, is in all his movies).<br /><br />At its peak, DSTV enjoyed a large and loyal following of 20 million fans. It hit the airwaves immediately following the close of two successful comedic monster-movie parodies, The Munsters (1964-1966) and The Addams Family (1964-1966). However, DSTV gained far higher ratings than either of those shows and ran over twice as long, perhaps because DSTV's approach to horror was melodramatic instead of comedic.<br /><br />The Munsters and The Addams Family can obviously be included in the rich tradition of horror parody in motion picture form, starting as early as the 1940's with movies such as Abbott and Costello Meet Frankenstein (1948), but many fans of DSTV, who regard the show with utmost seriousness, object to anyone characterizing DSTV as a monster parody. However, the stars of DSTV state that the show's attitude toward horror themes was actually, if not comedic, definitely impudent. Actor David Selby, who played Quentin in DSTV, states, \\"Fans who are upset [at Burton for creating a campy parody of the show] have to remember that when we made the series, we screwed with beloved monster movies and turned them upside down! We were hardly reverent ourselves.\\" This sentiment is seconded by Lara Parker, who played Angelique in DSTV.<br /><br />Burton shows his intention to honor DSTV, not mock it, by dedicating the film to Dan Curtis, DSTV's creator who is now deceased. In addition, Burton cast many of the stars of DSTV in cameos, including Frid, who died very recently at age 87, Selby, Parker, and Kathryn Leigh Scott, who played Josette. All of these actors have been strong supporters of the film.<br /><br />Burton also cast 90-year-old Christopher Lee in a small part in DS12, as a nod to Lee's famous portrayal of the prototype vampire from literature, [[ASIN:B000JQUBRM Count Dracula]], in multiple movies in the 60's and 70's, the same era as DSTV.<br /><br />To insure he had the best writer to achieve the approach he wanted to take with this film, Burton hired best-selling novelist Seth Grahame-Smith, author of [[ASIN:1594743347 Pride and Prejudice and Zombies]], to do the screenplay. The two of them have also worked together on another spoofy vampire movie, Abraham Lincoln: Vampire Hunter, which is set to release within a month of this movie, in June, 2012. Burton co-produced that film, which is based on a [[ASIN:1455510173 novel]] of the same name by Grahame-Smith, who wrote that film's screenplay as well.ined far higher ratings than either of those shows and ran over twice as long, perhaps because DSTV's approach to horror was melodramatic instead of comedic.<br /><br />The Munsters and The Addams Family can obviously be included in the rich tradition of horror parody in motion picture form, starting as early as the 1940's with movies such as Abbott and Costello Meet Frankenstein (1948), but many fans of DSTV, who regard the show with utmost seriousness, object to anyone characterizing DSTV as a monster parody. We were hardly reverent ourselves.\\" This sentiment is seconded by Lara Parker, who played Angelique in DSTV.<br /><br />Burton shows his intention to honor DSTV, not mock it, by dedicating the film to Dan Curtis, DSTV's creator who is now deceased. B004LAIU4I|2.0|Another carefully engineered musical extravaganza by the master of genre exploitation. While many seem to see Paul Simon as America's cultural ambassador to \\"world (and neglected) music\\", I see him as a vain emperor surrounding himself with exotic plunder for his personal glory. The sight of Simon trying to blues it up, backed up by genuine blues musicians on SNL, was sad and a bit infuriating. Simon is an honest-to-goodness musician, he's just not an honest one, who disguises his own limitations- all artists have them- in this case by placing himself as ringmaster in the middle of a multi-cultural circus. Though there's no denying the infectious melodies and sometimes poetry (sometimes someone else's, as in the case of, \\"Richard Cory\\") of their lyrics, I've always felt that the work of Simon and his partner was concocted- skillfully- rather than inspired- taking a little bit of this, and a little bit of that to make a song \\"work\\". Contrast this, for example, with the influence of American rhythm and blues on the early work of The Beatles. Blue-eyed soul or not, these were kids who were nuts about- even worshipful of- the music. I think that, even at the height of their career, they would never have presumed to \\"belong on the same stage with\\" Little Richard. My suspicions about Paul Simon as musical \\"survivor\\" were confirmed when I first heard the song, \\"American Tune\\", from the album, There Goes Rhymin' Simon. Simon was rhyming' off someone else's tune- namely, J. S. Bach- though that composer's name is nowhere to be found in the credits. Words and music by one Paul Simon! A Rolling Stone critic praised the song as \\"a flowing ballad with the chordal structure of an American hymn-tune\\"; Simon achieved the desired effect, and took the credit with no threat of a lawsuit from that dead German guy. (Yes, I know Bach's hymn was based on a secular song, hence the melody was not original; however, this was a common practice at the time, it was not assumed that tunes were original unless otherwise noted.)
B004MC8CA2|5.0|Great app.  this provides great devotionals, many versions of the bible for study and helps me study Gods word every day.
0060853972|4.0|The first time I read Good Omens, I must have been in my early teens and picked up the dog-eared, cracked-spine copy that was at the public library in Hout Bay. I still remember reading it in my mother's car while she went to check in on my grandparents at the retirement village. The book had been borrowed so often pages were falling out, and the library had tried to tape the volume together.<br /><br />I thought it would be an interesting exercise to trot this novel out again now that more than 15 years have passed since I last read it, and I must admit I have mixed feelings about the work in hindsight. First off, let me say that I absolutely adore Pratchett and Gaiman. I devoured piles of Discworld novels as a teen and Gaiman's Sandman graphic novels had a lasting impact on me. But something about Good Omens is just \\"off\\" for lack of better description.<br /><br />The main gripe I have is that the story suffers from \\"too much awesome\\". Pratchett and Gaiman shoehorned in too many elements which hampered the build-up of tension and resulted in an ending that went out with a whimper rather than a bang. This is the danger of having a large cast of characters and a multiple viewpoints written in an omniscient style. And it's exhausting keeping up with everyone, with scenes that flitter hither and thither.<br /><br />A big problem with this story, I feel, is the humour, which is possibly the reason why I failed to finish Douglas Adams's Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy series, and also can only stomach Pratchett's writing in isolated servings. Much like Monty Python and his ilk, I can only tolerate this sort of humour in small servings, and I always want it tempered by presenting it within a stronger narrative. I found the constant dry commentary exhausting after about a third of the novel and, consequently, struggled to finish reading all the way to the end. So yes, a large part of my feelings of meh-ness toward this novel lies with me. I don't think I am quite the reader for this novel.<br /><br />Granted, I did find the authors' characterisations amusing, but to get back to that comment of \\"too much awesome\\"... The novel loses steam early on so much so that I think the only way to really enjoy it is to read it in short bursts over a few weeks instead of an intense sitting.<br /><br />Good Omens is a stab at some of the classic 1980s horror, with a big nod to works such as The Omen. If you enjoyed that story, then you'll probably pick up a few of the hints tossed into Good Omens. The only character I felt an inkling of affection for was Adam and his dog, and feel if the story had been centred more around his doings and his gang, it might've had more impact. Some readers have complained that the story's \\"too preachy\\" and that it descends into moral nihilism. Maybe it's just that I tend toward nihilism at times that this didn't bother me too much. It's quite clear that far more people love this story than hate it, and it must be remembered that the reader is the final arbiter.<br /><br />At the end of the day, Good Omens gets bogged down by its large cast of characters, multiple story arcs and by its reliance on its own cleverness. I did appreciate the skits, which are Pythonesque at times, and this is not the best offering from either author. I can see where they come from, and probably conceived this novel out of a conversation where a number of \\"what ifs\\" were dropped. Granted, this is still a fun, light-hearted read which takes a stab at contemporary society and, for that alone, is worth reading. At no point did I feel like flinging the book across the room either, but I'll echo what a lot of other readers have said. If you want to see Gaiman or Pratchett at their best, rather look to staple fare such as the Discworld novels, the Sandman graphic novels and American Gods.der for this novel.<br /><br />Granted, I did find the authors' characterisations amusing, but to get back to that comment of \\"too much awesome\\"... It is more than a coffee table book with rich glossy pictures, and much more than a fan tribute with behind the scenes photos of filming on set.  While there are some comments from the actors to add some spice, Jessica Fellowes takes the same care and attention as her uncle Julian Fellowes to use the Crawley story line to pay tribute to the grand country houses like Highclere Castle where Downton is filmed. She teaches us about Edwardian life and the back story of the characters that we have come to know and love; while it was a different time, some things never change, like devotion to family.  It is like taking a history on the Edwardian era, using Downton Abbey as the teaching tool.  I would not be surprised if American universities will be launching courses using Downton as a backdrop.<br /><br />Julian Fellowes writes the Foreword as homage to these great houses which were mostly abandoned after the collapse of the agricultural economy.  Some were rescued and reinvented as new families used the grand space in different ways.  Sadly the vast kitchen and ante rooms behind the green baise door had to go.  An interesting tidbit was his reference to his great-aunt Isie who was the model for the Violet Grantham character.<br /><br />There are many books written about this era, some with connections to Downton Abbey which Amazon all carry, but this is a great one to start with.<br /><br />I covered this review on my own blog DowntonAbbeyCooks, dedicated to the food and recipes of Downton Abbey and the great Edwardian Era. Stuck in the bleak arctic, Ottway (Liam Neeson) is a sniper hired by an oil crew to protect them against the worst nature has. With his dark past very present in his mind, he boards a company airplane home with an oil crew as uncivilized as the landscape. After a random storm, the plane crashes in a frozen hell of ravenous wolves and unforgiving conditions. With only wits and ebbing resources, Ottway realizes they are being stalked by a pack of wolves. And it becomes a race between species to out-fight the other as the cold blizzard brings most certain death.<br /><br />And it is not just man against nature. It is man versus death.<br /><br />As the opening voice over narrates the futility of a life devoid of meaning, Ottway is preparing to commit suicide. With his heart broken and his spirit in pieces, The Grey begins where most stories about redemption end. Neeson, with his gravelly voice and sunken eyes, is the last candidate I expected to find in this story, touted as \\"Taken\\" with wolves. As advertised, The Grey was touted as Liam Neeson kicking ass and making slippers out of the remains of wolf.<br /><br />Instead, we get a soulful and complex figure haunted by the demons of the past, as well as the specter of death, and Neeson transcends his own acting action film persona to give us a character who is both fragile and strong. A man who bleeds and begs of the Divine to save him. The scene that involves Neeson literally screaming and cursing the Divine is both chilling in it's realism and horrifying when we realize that he might actually be right. The scene is so profound when one thinks about the personal background of Neeson, who lost his wife several years before.<br /><br />When taken in that way, the scene that has now become inescapably haunting in my mind is all the more visceral and real. I wasn't certain it was Ottway who was screaming at the Divine, but Neeson. Though, I've heard that Neeson is a Roman Catholic, even the best of us find it crushing to even consider the hand of the Divine in the midst of unspeakable tragedy.<br /><br />Indeed, death gets quite a bit of screen time, both in conversation and action, with the wolves manipulating the group, and the fights are sparse but vicious and brutal. But that is not the point of the story, and director Joe Carnahan (The A-Team) doesn't dwell on the gore, only using it to point to the wider philosophical spectrum. The musical score is sparse, and allows the film to set it's own pace, which allows for the silences to speak louder about the characters. There are scenes of graphic violence involving people's in a state of shock being ripped apart, but the images are often beautiful in their transcendent power. The violence is not the point.<br /><br />Death, and how we deal with it, is the point. It is cold, calculating, and inescapable. Like God's judgment, there is nothing we can do to be free of it's curse. At least, for now.<br /><br />I sat on my couch for three hours last night, pondering the philosophical concepts of The Grey. I didn't remember the blood, the jump scenes, the scenery, the music or the acting; though all of that is outstanding. But, The Grey is not about the wolves, or even about Liam Neeson. It is first and foremost a story about God, and where He is when suffering bares it's fangs. The film is not necessarily interested in answering the provocative questions it presents, nor should it, but it presents them in the midst of transcendent emotion and complex spirituality.<br /><br />The ending is akin to The Sopranos, and it will divide audiences. I personally felt it ended on a pitch-perfect note, both ambiguous and gripping. It allows it's themes to fully remain intact without succumbing to cliche, and frankly, it is refreshing to see a film end a film on it's own terms, rather than on the expectations of it's audience.<br /><br />The twist that is revealed will not rock your world,but the climactic build up to it had tears in my eyes. It forces us to look beyond the present state of chaos and focus on what gives life meaning. And does that meaning involving more than an instinct for survival?<br /><br />Once more into the fray<br />Into the last good fight I'll ever know<br />Live and die on this day<br />Live and die on this day<br /><br />Come for the wolves, stay for the rest. 1591391105|5.0|I've now read this twice and recommended it many more times to colleagues who have been promoted into engineering management. I can't say that it will have the same impact on everyone, but engineers seem to find these analytic methods easy to digest. In particular I like the idea of dividing your conversations with your new boss into distinct conversations. They start with questions to learn situational awareness (is this a turn around or a continuing success situation?), and save the conversation about resources that the boss will provide (like career guidance) until later. Save yourself a trip through the mine field, and read this as soon as you can after your promotion.

B0039UTDFG|4.0|I own most every BBC Earth nature documentary and I appreciate what goes in to making these incredible films.  \\"Life\\" is an incredible series and I highly recommend to everyone, but the version narrated by David Attenborough.  I was born and raised in the U.S. but the Americanized versions of the BBC Earth documentaries (for the most part) are terrible due to the choice of narrator.  They typically are not knowledgeable about the material, and make the video almost unbearable (e.g., Oprah, Alex Baldwin in Frozen Planet, Mike Rowe in Human Planet, etc.).  The actual video content is truly incredible and anyone interested in life on Earth should purchase Life narrated by David Attenborough.  This goes for every BBC Earth documentary.  If possible (exception is Human Planet narrated by John Hurt who does a good job) get the version narrated by David Attenborough.
B006ISJQ26|5.0|Brian DePalma was considered a master plagiarist by many.  It's with little irony that \\"London Boulevard\\" reminds you of DePalma's \\"Carlito's Way\\".  Director William Monahan also references Martin Scorsese which also ironically Monahan wrote the Oscar winning script for \\"The Departed\\" which was a reimagining of the Hong Kong crime thriller \\"Infernal Affairs\\".  The question you ask yourself is Monahan's effort more than the sum of it's influences?  The answer to me is a resounding yes.  Similarities to other films cannot be denied but when done right you call it a homage.  Monahan introduces enough unique fluorishes and twists to elevate it above mere copycat status.  Colin Farrell is magnetic and macho as the recently released con who wants to go straight in a world that keeps pulling him into a life of crime.  Ray Winstone adds another indelible portrait to his personal rogues gallery as a sadistic crime boss.  David Thewlis nearly steals the show as the foppish manager of Keira Knightley's papparazzi stalked movie starlet.  The film kept me rapt and invigorated throughout.  For those who would dismiss \\"London Boulevard\\" as derivative I ask you to consider Bogart, Cagney, and Edward G.  They were gangster archetypes who didn't play the same character twice.  \\"London Boulevard\\" is a variation of the British gangster tradition that includes \\"Get Carter\\", \\"The Long Good Friday\\", \\"Mona Lisa\\" and \\"Sexy Beast'.

Then I thought about the cover and the name combined and was totally sold on buying his set of books.  You don't come across brilliant covers and names of books very often, so I decided to buy them both and check them out.  Well I went to the publisher site and bought the hard covers, so i cannot comment on the quality of the paperback books, but the hard cover books are quite excellent.<br /><br />At first I was quite impressed with the introduction, but got a little worried when i started the Knight Fork section cause it was definitely for beginners.  However as i proceeded to read the book, there were tons of little gold nuggets on how to think and what to look for that you do not find in other tactics books - at least not the ones that i have. The positions start out very simple and slowly progress to more and more difficult levels.  What I am finding is that the slow progression and excellently chosen positions that he uses to explain his message, are helping me retain the ideas, unlike most tactic books where you are supposed to somehow learn subconscious patterns which might help a little because solving problems or puzzles in practice makes perfect. I believe in total there is 56 pages in the Double Attack section on Knight Forks alone. There is over 700 pages of positions, explanations, and little nuggets of gold throughout the book.  These are NOT!!! your normal tactics books where you solve a bunch of puzzles and forget them all in a few days.
